Hooker\n\nTechnical\n\nReport\n\nSeries\n\nThe NASA\n\nSTI Program\n\nSince its founding, NASA has been dedicated\nthe advancement\nof aeronautics\nand space\nscience. The NASA Scientific and Technical\n\nto\n\nInformation\n(STI) Program Office plays a key\npart in helping NASA maintain this important\nrole.\nThe NASA STI Program Office is operated by\nLangley Research Center, the lead center for\nNASA\'s scientific and technical information.\nThe NASA STI Program Office provides access\nto the NASA STI Database, the largest collection\nof aeronautical\nand space science STI in the\nworld. The Program Office is also NASA\'s\ninstitutional\nmechanism\nfor disseminating\nthe\nresults of its research and development\nactivities. These results are published by NASA in the\nNASA STI Report Series, which includes the\nfollowing report types:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 TECHNICAL\nPUBLICATION.\nReports of\ncompleted\nresearch or a major significant\nphase of research that present the results of\nNASA programs and include extensive data or\ntheoretical\nanalysis. Includes compilations\nof\nsignificant\nscientific and technical data and\ninformation\ndeemed to be of continuing\nreference value. NASA\'s counterpart\nof\npeer-reviewed\nformal professional\npapers but\nhas less stringent limitations\non manuscript\nlength and extent of graphic presentations.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 TECHNICAL\n\nMEMORANDUM.\n\nScientific\n\nand technical findings that are preliminary\nor\nof specialized\ninterest, e.g., quick release\nreports, working papers, and bibliographies\nthat contain minimal annotation.\nDoes not\ncontain\n\nextensive\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CONTRACTOR\n\nanalysis.\nREPORT.\n\nScientific\n\ntechnical findings by NASA-sponsored\ncontractors\nand grantees.\n\nand\n\nOffice\n\n... in Profile\n\nCONFERENCE\n\nPUBLICATION.\n\nCollected\n\npapers from scientific and technical\nconferences,\nsymposia,\nseminars, or other\nmeetings sponsored\nor cosponsored\nby NASA.\nSPECIAL PUBLICATION.\nScientific, technical, or historical\ninformation\nfrom NASA\nprograms,\nprojects, and mission, often concerned with subjects having substantial\npublic\ninterest.\nTECHNICAL\n\nTRANSLATION.\n\nEnglish-language\ntific and technical\nmission.\n\ntranslations\nof foreign scienmaterial pertinent to NASA\'s\n\nSpecialized\nservices that complement\nthe STI\nProgram Office\'s diverse offerings include creating custom thesauri, building customized\ndatabases, organizing and publishing research results...\neven providing videos.\nFor more information about the NASA STI Program\nOffice, see the following:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Access the NASA STI Program Home\nhttp://www.sti.nasa.gov/STI-homepage.html\n\xe2\x80\xa2 E-mail your question\nhelp@sti.nasa.gov\n\nvia the Internet\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Fax your question to the NASA\nDesk at (301) 621-0134\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Telephone\nthe NASA\n(301) 621-0390\nWrite\n\nAccess\n\nPage at\n\nto\n\nAccess\n\nHelp\n\nHelp Desk at\n\nto:\n\nNASA Access Help Desk\nNASA Center for AeroSpace\nInformation\n800 Elkridge Landing Road\nLinthicum\nHeights, MD 21090-2934\n\nNASA/TM-1998-104566,\n\nSeaWiFS\nStanford\nGoddard\nElaine\nGeneral\n\nTechnical\n\nB. It was\noriginated\nas a cooperative\neffort between the government\nand industry,\nand had a spacecraft\nlaunch date of 31\nJuly 1993. In this case, GSFC and OSC would share the costs of the mission.\nGSFC would specify the data that\nwas needed and buy the research\nrights to these data, maintaining\ninsight, but not oversight\nrights, with their\nindustrial\npartner.\nGSFC would also provide calibration\nand validation\nfor these data. OSC would provide the\nspacecraft\nand instrument,\nlaunch services,\nand spacecraft\noperations\nto provide data for five years at a fixed\nprice of $43 million. OSC would retain the operational\nand commercial\nrights to these data. In order to protect\nOSC\'s data rights, research data release would be delayed, unless timely release is necessary\nfor calibration\nand\nvalidation\nactivities.\nBecause\nof the focus on data products,\nthe Project\nstructure\nIt is housed within the Earth science organization,\nwhere the\nof the engineering\nsupport\nis matrL,:ed into the organization\n\nis different\nfrom classic flight projects\nat GSFC.\nmajority\nof the staff are scientists.\nThe majority\non an as-needed\nbasis.\nDuring the development\n\nand early operations\nphase, the Project\nwas under team leadership\nby the Project\nManager\n(an engineer),\nand\nthe Project\nScientist\n(an oceanographer).\nAfter the spacecraft\nwas launched\nand it entered routine operations,\nthe Project\nmanagement\nwas turned over to the Project\nScientist.\nData collection\nis specified by the Mission\nOperations\nElement,\nwho control the SeaWiFS\ninstrument\non the spacecraft.\nThe global data are received at\nWallops Flight Facility and are then transferred\nto GSFC. At GSFC, the Data Processing\nElement\nreceives these\ndata and generates\nstandard\nglobal ocean color data products.\nThis process includes calibration\nand validation\nof these data and quality assurance,\nwhich is provided\nby the Calibration\nand Validation\nElement,\nwhich also\nincludes\na Field Program\nfor in situ work. Local area coverage data and back-up\nglobal data are also collected\nat GSFC. The Project\nOffice Staff provide support\nand a buffer for the technical\nstaff. The Project\nOffice is\nvirtually\nlocated on the World Wide Web, and that has made coordination\nof a global project infinitely\neasier.\nThe original\nschedule\nspecified\ncertified\ndata delivery by December\n1, 1993. During spacecraft\ndevelopment,\nnumerous\ndelays were encountered,\nand data delivery was delayed\nuntil December\n20, 1997.\nThe delay was\nextremely\npainful for everyone involved,\nbut it did allow for significant\nrefinement\nand documentation\nof our\nwork.\nThis prelaunch\ntechnical\nmemorandum\nseries will conclude\nwith this 43rd volume,\nconsiderably\nlarger\nthan was originally\nanticipated.\nThe excellence\nof the series was recognized\nby a NASA Group Achievement\nAward presented\nto the Series Editors,\nStanford\nB. Hooker and Elaine R. Firestone.\nAlthough\nthe instrument\nwas optimized\nfor ocean imaging,\nthe SeaWiFS\ninstrument\nwas modified\nto decrease\nstray light effects.\nThat\nchange allowed the instrument\nto produce\ngood land imagery as well. With the addition\nof the land data, the\nProject\nthat was tasked\nwith providing\nregular\nglobal ocean color data, was able to produce\nregular\nglobal\nbiospheric\ndata for the first time in history.\nThe Project\nthanks\neveryone\nwho invested\ntheir\nthese data will hopefully\nexceed all expectations\nthe development\nphase.\n"With that said, I will now turn\nbeen a pleasure\nand an inspiration\n\nGreenbelt,\nFebruary\n\ntime and energy in this\nthose same expectations\n\nthe SeaWiFS\nProject\nover to the\nto work with all of you."\n\nProject\n\neffort.\nThe research\nthat kept everyone\n\nScientist,\n\nChuck\n\nfacilitated\nby\ngoing through\n\nMcClain.\n\nM.L.\n\nIt has\n\nMaryland\n1998\n\nProject\n\nii\n\nCleave\nManager\n\nE.R.Firestone ndS.B.Hooker\na\n\nABSTRACT\nTheSea-viewing\nWideField-of-view\nSensor\n(SeaWiFS) thefollow-on\nis\nocean\ncolorinstrumento the Coastal\nt\nZoneColorScannerCZCS), hichceasedperations 1986,afteran eight-year\n(\nw\no\nin\nmission.SeaWiFS\nwas\nlaunched n 1 August1997, n the OrbView-2\no\no\nsatellite, uilt by OrbitalSciences\nb\nCorporation\n(OSC).The\nSeaWiFSrojectat theNational eronautics\nP\nA\nandSpace dministrationNASA)\nA\n(\nGoddard pace lightCenter\nS\nF\n(GSFC),\nundertookheresponsibility documenting aspects thismission,\nt\nof\nall\nof\nwhich criticalto the ocean\nis\ncolorandmarine\nscienceommunities.\nc\nThis documentation,\nentitledthe Sea\nWiFS Technical\nReport\nSeries,\nis in the form of NASA Technical\nMemorandum\nNumber\n104566 and 1998 104566.\nAll reports\npublished\nare\nvolumes within the series. This particular\nvolume, which is the last of the so-called\nPrela_mch Series serves as a\nreference,\nor guidebook,\nto the previous 42 volumes and consists of 6 sections including:\nan addenda,\nan errata,\nan index to key words and phrases,\nlists of acronyms\nand symbols used, and a list of all references\ncited. Remote\nSaskatoon,\nSaskatchewan,\nCanadian\nRemote\ning Society, Canadian\nAeronautics\nand Space\ntute, Ottawa,\nOntario,\n357 362.\n\n3.\n\nCharrefactivSens.,\nSensInsti-\n\nADDENDA\n\nThis section presents\na summary\nof the SeaWiFS\nBiooptical\nAlgorithm\nMini-workshop\n(SeaBAM)\nwhich was\nheld 21 24 January\n1997; submitted\nby C. McClain.\nIn\naddition,\nit presents\na summary\nof the proceedings\nfrom\nthe Second SeaWiFS\nScience Team Meeting\nheld at the\nOmni Hotel in Baltimore,\nMaryland,\n6 8 January\n1998.\n\n3.1\n\nSeaBAM\n\nAbstract\n\nOne of the primary\ngoals of the SeaWiFS\nProject\nis to\nroutinely\ngenerate\nglobal chlorophyll\na and Coastal\nZone\nColor Scanner (CZCS) pigment concentrations\nwith an accuracy of +35% (Hooker et al. 1992). Since its inception\nin 1991, the SeaWiFS\nCalibration\nand Validation\nProgram\nhas undertaken\na number of initiatives\nto help ensure that\nthis goal is met, e.g., measurement\nprotocol\ndevelopment,\ncalibration\nround-robins,\na bio-optical\ndata archive,\nand\nbio-optical\nalgorithm\nworkshops.\nAfter the seventh\nbiooptical\nalgorithm\nworkshop\nheld in Halifax,\nNova Scotia\non 21 October\n1996, it was clear that algorithm\nand data\nquality issues remained\nthat could not be adequately\naddressed\nin the standard\nworkshop\nformat.\nA more interactive analysis\n(data sets and algorithms)\nworkshop\nwas\n\n4\n\nIndex\n\ndeemed\nnecessary\nin order to focus on specific problems.\nAs a result, the first SeaWiFS Bio-optical\nAlgorithm\nMiniworkshop\n(SeaBAM)\nwas hosted by D. Siegel at the University of California\nat Santa Barbara\n(UCSB) during 21\n24 January\n1997. This chapter provides an overview of the\nworkshop\nbackground,\norganization,\napproach,\nand results\nfrom the workshop\nand other associated\nactivities.\n3.1.1\n\nProctor,\nJ., and Y.P. Barnes, 1996: NIST High Accuracy\nReference\nReflectometer-spectrophotometer.\nJ. Res. Natl. Inst. Stand. Teehnol., 101, (accepted).\n\nCumulative\n\nIntroduction\n\nThe rationale\nbehind the CZCS pigment product\n(chlorophyll a plus phaeophytin)\nis to provide a data set that\ncan be compared\nto products\nderived from CZCS for studies of decadal\nscale variability.\nEarly comparisons\nof in\nsitu and CZCS global products\n(Balch et al. 1992) indicared that this goal was feasible\nfor most of the global\nocean. Subsequent\nstudies, however, noted significant\ndiPferences\neven in clear water environments\n(Arrigo\net al.\n1994). The CZCS algorithm\nwas based on 55 bio-optical\nstations\nin coastal US waters (Clark 1981; usually referred\nto as the Nimbus\nExperiment\nTeam, or NET, data set)\nand even by 1991, that data set was the only data set generally available\nfor algorithm\ndevelopment.\nAs a result,\nthe SeaWiFS\nCalibration\nand\nated several activities\ndirected\nof bio-optical\ndata collected\nby\nnity. These activities\nincluded\n\nValidation\nProgram\ninitiat improving\nthe quality\nthe ocean optics commuthe establishment\nof mea-\n\nsurement\nprotocols\n(Mueller\nand Austin 1992 and 1995),\nthe SeaWiFS\nIntercalibration\nRound-Robin\nExperiments\n(SIRREXs,\ne.g., Johnson\net al. 1996), the SeaWiFS\nBiooptical Archive and Storage System (SeaBASS;\nHooker et\nal. 1994), the SeaWiFS\nTransfer\nRadiometer\n(SXR) and\nthe SeaWiFS\nQuality\nMonitor\n(SQM, Shaw et al. 1996,\nHooker and Aiken 1998, and Johnson\net al. 1998). In addition,\nseven bio-optical\nalgorithm\nworkshops\nhave been\nheld, brief proceedings\nof which are published\nin the SeaWiFS Technical Report Series cumulative\nindexes Volumes\n12, 18, 24, and 36 (Firestone\nand Hooker 1993, 1994, 1995,\nand 1996).\nThe bio-optical\nalgorithm\nworkshops\nhave been open\nevents and have provided\na forum for presentation\nand\ndetailed\ndiscussions\non protocols,\ndata collection,\nand algorithm issues. Early in the deliberations\non algorithm\ndevelopment,\nit was decided\nto avoid switching\nalgorithms,\nsuch as was used in the global CZCS reprocessing\n(Gordon et al. 1983) and to use a semi-analytical\nchlorophyll\nalgorithm.\nSwitching\nalgorithms\ntend to produce\nbimodal\nfrequency\ndistributions\nas an artifact\nof the switching logic\n(Denman\nand Abbott 1988 and Miiller-Karger\net al. 1990).\nSemi-analytical\nalgorithms\n(Carder et al. 1991, Garver and\nSiegel 1997, and Carder\net al. 1997) would allow more\nphysical\ninsight into the optical processes\nthat determine\noceanic reflectance,\nthus providing\na mechanism\nfor incorporating\nvariability\n\nstrategies\nto account\nin the algorithm.\n\nfor regional\n\nand\n\ntemporal\n\nE.R.\nThe\ntive\n\ndiscussions\n\nat the\n\nin highlighting\n\nproaches\n\nto\n\nproduct,\n\nfor\n\nthe\n\nraised\n\njust\n\na, why\n\nthe\n\nCZCS\n\na Carder\n\n3.1.3\n\nIndex\n\nwith California\n\nState Polytechnic\n\nApproach\n\nGiven that the primary\nobjective\nwas to finalize and\ndeliver the operational\nSeaWiFS\nchlorophyll\na and CZCS\npigment algorithms\nin a relatively short time, it was agreed\nby the participants\n(Table 1) that a successful\nworkshop\nwould require a substantial\namount\nof pre-workshop\npreparation, and the free and rapid dissemination\nof data, code,\nand results.\nIt also required\nthat all those involved\nbe\nwilling to assist one another,\nwork out problems\nand differences\nof opinion\ninternally,\nand to recognize\nthat\nall\noptions\nwere open with regard to the final algorithm\nselections.\nThe strategy\nwas to have a balanced,\nbut small,\ngroup of participants,\nincluding\nrepresentatives\nfrom the\nSeaWiFS\nProject\nand others who had been active in the\nbio-optics\nsubgroup\nas algorithm\ndevelopers\n(empirical\nand\nsemi-analytic),\nand bio-optical\ndata providers.\nIt was also\nagreed that D. Siegel would host the workshop\nat UCSB\nand that he would assume responsibility\nfor providing\na\nworkshop\nenvironment\nthat efficiently accommodated\nboth\ngroup discussion\nand presentation\nsessions and a heterogenous computing\nand data storage\nenvironment.\nGSFC\nwould coordinate\npre- and post-workshop\nactivities\nincluding maintenance\nof an access-restricted\nSeaBAM\nweb page\nwhere data, results,\nand electronic\nmail (e-mail) would be\nposted\nand archived.\nBoth S. If other researchers\nwould\nlike to be a participant\nin this Working Group, please send\nan e-mail message to A. Thomas\n(thomas@maine\xe2\x80\xa2maine\n\xe2\x80\xa2 edu) with a brief note including:\nname, address,\ntelephone number,\ne-mail address,\nand URL, along with a few\nnotes (in bullet-form)\non the title, goals, and focus of the\nocean color related research.\nIn addition,\nthe researchers\nshould send a few notes on the approach,\nand the satellite data or ocean color related data sets they have, or will\ncreate.\nThe current\nmembers\nare W. Balch, J. Bisagni,\nJ.\nIrish, B. Monger, J. O\'Reilly,\nJ. Salisbury,\nA. Thomas,\nand\nC. Yentsch.\n\nAs\n\ninitial\n\ngoals,\n\nthe\n\nfollowing\n\nmodest\n\nstrawmen\n\nwere\n\nposed:\n\na)\n\nTo identify the community\ncarrying out ocean\nrelated research\nin the Gulf of Maine;\n\nb)\n\nTo communicate\nthe goals and\nrespective\nresearch\nefforts; and\n\nc)\n\nTo identify\nactivities.\n\navailable\n\ndata\n\nsets\n\napproaches\nand\n\ndata\n\ncolor\n\nof their\nprocessing\n\nThese are just a starting\npoint.\nInput is welcome and\nencouraged.\nThe Working\nGroup can be as active or as\ninactive\nas the members\nchoose.\nThe SeaWiFS\nProject\nis simply looking for mechanisms\nto maximize\nthe scientific output and productivity\nresulting\nfrom satellite ocean\ncolor data.\nA World Wide Web site has been established\nas a point\nof reference\nand communication.\nThrough\nthis site, the\nmembers will first aim at the above goals and proceed from\nthere. ABSTRACT\n\n(Maximum\n\n200\n\nwords)\n\nThe Sea-viewing Wide Field-of-view\nSensor (SeaWiFS) is the follow-on ocean color instrument to the Coastal\nZone Color Scanner (CZCS), which ceased operations in 1986, after an eight-year mission. SeaWiFS was\nlaunched on 1 August 1997, on the SeaStar satellite, built by Orbital Sciences Corporation (OSC). The\nSeaWiFS Project at the National Aeronautics and Space Administration\n(NASA) Goddard Space Flight Center\n(GSFC), undertook the responsibility\nof documenting all aspects of this mission, which is critical to the ocean\ncolor and marine science communities.\nThis documentation,\nentitled the SeaWiFS Technical Report Series, is\nin the form of NASA Technical Memorandum\nNumber 104566 and 1998-104566.\nAll reports published are\nvolumes within the series. This particular volume, which is the last of the so-called Prelaunch Series serves as\na reference, or guidebook, to the previous 42 volumes and consists of 6 sections including: an addenda, an\nerrata, an index to key words and phrases, lists of acronyms and symbols used, and a list of all references cited.\nThe editors have published a cumulative index of this type after every five volumes. Each index covers the\nreference topics published in all previous editions, that is, each new index includes all of the information\ncontained in the preceeding indexes with the exception of any addenda\n14.
